<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">London Overground</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<p>The <b>London Overground</b> (also known as <b>the Overground</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Urban_rail_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Urban rail in the United Kingdom">suburban rail</a> network in the United Kingdom. Established in 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TfL_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-TfL-4">[4]</a></sup> it serves a large part of <a href="/wiki/Greater_London" title="Greater London">Greater London</a> and parts of <a href="/wiki/Hertfordshire" title="Hertfordshire">Hertfordshire</a>, with 112 stations on several routes. The network forms part of the <a href="/wiki/National_Rail" title="National Rail">National Rail</a> network, but under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_franchising_in_Great_Britain" title="Rail franchising in Great Britain">franchise</a> control and branding of <a href="/wiki/Transport_for_London" title="Transport for London">Transport for London</a> (TfL). Operation has been franchised to <a href="/wiki/Arriva_Rail_London" title="Arriva Rail London">Arriva Rail London</a> since 13 November 2016.</p>
<p>The Overground has been assigned the colour orange as a mode specific colour by Transport for London. This colour is used in the Overground version of the TfL roundel, for the representation of Overground routes on the <a href="/wiki/Tube_map" title="Tube map">tube map</a>, in train interiors and elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tflcs4_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-tflcs4-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:London_Overground_as_a_circle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1240" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/London_Overground_as_a_circle.svg/250px-London_Overground_as_a_circle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/London_Overground_as_a_circle.svg/375px-London_Overground_as_a_circle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/London_Overground_as_a_circle.svg/500px-London_Overground_as_a_circle.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:London_Overground_as_a_circle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Unofficial London Overground circular representation</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Rail services in <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain" title="Rail transport in Great Britain">Great Britain</a> are mostly run under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_franchising_in_Great_Britain" title="Rail franchising in Great Britain">franchises</a> operated by private train operating companies, marketed together as National Rail.</p>
<p>The concept of developing a network of orbital services around London goes back to the independently produced Ringrail proposals in the early 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Some of these were evaluated in the London Rail Study of 1974 (The Barren Report) and Barren suggested consideration of a North London Network of orbital services, based on a later suggestion by the Ringrail Group, which involved using many existing rail routes, rather than new construction suggested in earlier drafts of the Ringrail Plan.</p>
<p>The proposal from Barren was for several overlapping services mainly using the North London Line, generally at 20-minute intervals. The suggested routes followed the original North London Line service from Broad Street to Richmond, and new services from Barking to Clapham Junction, and a third service from Ealing Broadway to North Woolwich. However, the continuing antipathy to the railways from the third Wilson government,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> along with the lack of interest in minor local train services by British Railways' management, meant that few of these initiatives were carried forward.</p>
<p>In 1979, the then GLC decided to sponsor an improved service from Camden Road, on the North London Line, to North Woolwich, opening up a previously freight-only line between Dalston and Stratford and linking it to an improved Stratford – North Woolwich service. This was given the marketing name 'Cross Town Link-Line', and operated with basic 2-car diesel units.</p>
<p>The next initiative came from the GLC in 1984, when the government supported the Broadgate development that would entail the demolition of Broad Street Station. The closure process was convoluted because of problems in making alternative arrangements for the North London Line, and the remaining services operating from Watford to the City. These would eventually run to and from Liverpool Street via a new section of track, the Graham Road Curve.</p>
<p>Nevertheless, the new Richmond to North Woolwich service quickly settled down, but the then British Rail made a serious management error replacing the existing 3-car <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_501" title="British Rail Class 501">Class 501</a> electric trains (built 1957<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>) with slightly newer but shorter 2-car <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_416" title="British Rail Class 416">Class 416</a> electric trains (built 1959<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>), that quickly became overcrowded. In 1988, by reorganising and reducing services on the Great Northern routes from Moorgate, about 18 relatively modern <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_313" title="British Rail Class 313">Class 313</a> dual-voltage electric trains were transferred to operate the North London and Watford services, from both Euston and Liverpool Street.</p>
<p>Several voluntary sector groups, the Railway Development Society (RDS), later Railfuture, Transport 2000's then London groups, and the Capital Transport Campaign, launched a series of leaflets and briefings promoting a concept called Outer Circle. This used a name once associated with a semicircular service that operated from Broad Street to Mansion House, but ceased during World War 1.</p>
<p>The pamphlets and briefings, first issued in 1997, initially suggested a route from Clapham Junction to the Greenwich Peninsula, intended to improve access from south London to the Millennium Dome. However, this was thwarted by architect <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rogers" title="Richard Rogers">Richard Rogers</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> who considered a railway route on an elevated viaduct could cause 'community severance'<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and so the Victorian brick viaduct was demolished. Nothing further happened to develop this network until after the new GLA was set up in 2000. But the lobbying discreetly continued with a series of short briefings published by one RDS member based in North London. Mayoral and GLA candidates were approached to discuss the viability of the Outer Circle concept. The principle was widely supported and was adopted into the first Mayor's Transport Plan, published in 2001.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, a pilot scheme was launched in 2003 to bring several National Rail local services, mainly in South London and operated by <a href="/wiki/Category:Railway_operators_in_London" title="Category:Railway operators in London">multiple companies</a>, under the <a href="/wiki/Overground_Network" title="Overground Network">ON – Overground Network</a> brand. TfL introduced consistent information displays, station signage and maps on the selected routes in South London. Although this pilot was primarily an exercise in branding, some service improvements were introduced, and it was the first instance of the newly created TfL having a visible influence over National Rail services. The pilot scheme was later dropped.</p>
<p>In January 2004 the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Transport" title="Department for Transport">Department for Transport</a> announced a review of the rail industry in Great Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> As part of that review, proposals were put forward by TfL for a "London Regional Rail Authority" to give TfL regulatory powers over rail services in and around Greater London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LRRA_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-LRRA-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>A result of this consultation was agreement by the then Secretary of State for Transport, Alistair Darling, to transfer part of the <a href="/wiki/Silverlink" title="Silverlink">Silverlink Metro Group of services</a> rail franchise to TfL control. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Silverlink had two areas of operation: Silverlink County regional services from <a href="/wiki/Euston_railway_station" title="Euston railway station">Euston</a> to <a href="/wiki/Northampton_railway_station" title="Northampton railway station">Northampton</a>, <a href="/wiki/St_Albans_Abbey_railway_station" title="St Albans Abbey railway station">St Albans Abbey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bletchley_railway_station" title="Bletchley railway station">Bletchley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bedford_railway_station" title="Bedford railway station">Bedford</a>; and Silverlink Metro within the London urban area. When the franchise was split up in 2007, County services were taken over by the <a href="/wiki/London_Midland" title="London Midland">London Midland</a> franchise,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> and the Metro services came under TfL control. TfL decided to let this franchise as a management contract, with TfL taking the revenue risk.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_announcements">Initial announcements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Initial announcements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 20 February 2006, the Department for Transport announced that TfL would take over management of services then provided by <a href="/wiki/Silverlink" title="Silverlink">Silverlink Metro</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansard2006_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-hansard2006-13">[13]</a></sup> Tenders were invited to operate the service under the provisional name of the <a href="/wiki/North_London_Railway" title="North London Railway">North London Railway</a>. On 5 September 2006, London Overground branding was announced, and it was confirmed that the extended East London Line would be included.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch">Launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:London_Overground_2007.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="3204" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/London_Overground_2007.png/220px-London_Overground_2007.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/London_Overground_2007.png/330px-London_Overground_2007.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/London_Overground_2007.png/440px-London_Overground_2007.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:London_Overground_2007.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Initial London Overground network from November 2007 (orange) and the East London line in 2010 (light orange).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007networkmap_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-2007networkmap-14">[14]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 11 November 2007, TfL took over the franchise for <i><a href="/wiki/North_London_Railway" title="North London Railway">North London Railway</a></i> routes, formerly Silverlink Metro.</p>
<p>The official launch ceremony was on 12 November 2007 at <a href="/wiki/Hampstead_Heath_railway_station" title="Hampstead Heath railway station">Hampstead Heath station</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_London" title="Mayor of London">Mayor of London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ken_Livingstone" title="Ken Livingstone">Ken Livingstone</a>, with a later media event on the bay platform at <a href="/wiki/Willesden_Junction_station" title="Willesden Junction station">Willesden Junction</a>.</p>
<p>The launch was accompanied by a marketing campaign entitled "London's new train set", with posters and leaflets carrying an image of <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_modelling" title="Rail transport modelling">model railway</a> packaging containing new Overground trains, tracks and staff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trainset_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-trainset-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the launch, TfL undertook to revamp the routes by improving service frequencies, staffing all stations, improving station facilities, introducing new rolling stock and allowing <a href="/wiki/Oyster_card_(pay_as_you_go)_on_National_Rail" title="Oyster card (pay as you go) on National Rail">Oyster pay as you go</a> throughout the network from the outset.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buildingLO_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-buildingLO-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>All stations were "deep-cleaned"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-all_change_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-all_change-17">[17]</a></sup> following the TfL takeover, and Silverlink branding removed. Station signage was replaced with Overground-branded signs using TfL's corporate <a href="/wiki/Johnston_(typeface)" title="Johnston (typeface)">New Johnston</a> typeface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buildingLO_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-buildingLO-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-all_change_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-all_change-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 15 April 2009 the North London Line platforms at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stratford_(Regional)_station" title="Stratford (Regional) station">Stratford</a> moved to new high-level platforms 1 &amp; 2 from low-level platforms 1 &amp; 2, the latter being needed for the DLR's <a href="/wiki/Stratford_International_station" title="Stratford International station">Stratford International</a> service. Platforms 1 &amp; 2 comprise an island platform with step-free access to platform 12 and subway links to platforms 3–11.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 27 September 2009, <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Wharf_railway_station" title="Imperial Wharf railway station">Imperial Wharf station</a> opened on the West London Line, between <a href="/wiki/West_Brompton_station" title="West Brompton station">West Brompton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clapham_Junction_railway_station" title="Clapham Junction railway station">Clapham Junction</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_London_Line_extension">East London Line extension</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: East London Line extension">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/East_London_Line" title="East London Line">East London Line</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Overground_Shoreditch_bridge_AB2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Overground_Shoreditch_bridge_AB2.JPG/200px-Overground_Shoreditch_bridge_AB2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Overground_Shoreditch_bridge_AB2.JPG/300px-Overground_Shoreditch_bridge_AB2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Overground_Shoreditch_bridge_AB2.JPG/400px-Overground_Shoreditch_bridge_AB2.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Overground_Shoreditch_bridge_AB2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The new Overground rail bridge is lowered into place over Shoreditch High Street</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 27 April 2010, the East London Line became part of the London Overground network when the Phase 1 extension was completed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ell-april2010_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-ell-april2010-19">[19]</a></sup> The former London Underground line was extended northwards, mostly along the former <a href="/wiki/Broad_Street_railway_station_(London)" title="Broad Street railway station (London)">Broad Street</a> viaduct of the North London Line, to the re-opened <a href="/wiki/Dalston_Junction_railway_station" title="Dalston Junction railway station">Dalston Junction</a>, and southwards to <a href="/wiki/Crystal_Palace_railway_station" title="Crystal Palace railway station">Crystal Palace</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Croydon_station" title="West Croydon station">West Croydon</a>.</p>
<p>Operations began with a limited preview service between Dalston Junction and New Cross/New Cross Gate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new_era_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-new_era-20">[20]</a></sup> with full operation between Dalston Junction and West Croydon/Crystal Palace on 23 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_full_service_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_full_service-21">[21]</a></sup> On 28 February 2011, the line between Dalston Junction and Highbury &amp; Islington was opened. In attendance were the <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_London" title="Mayor of London">Mayor of London</a> and <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a>'s Managing Director. TfL announced in November 2010 that ridership was ahead of forecast at 92,000 a day, and that patronage at Surrey Quays had "gone through the roof".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-champion_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-champion-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The incorporation of the East London Line into the Overground network has added substantial sections of line in tunnel, including the historic <a href="/wiki/Thames_Tunnel" title="Thames Tunnel">Thames Tunnel</a>, the oldest tunnel under a navigable river in the world. A peculiarity is that at <a href="/wiki/Whitechapel_station" title="Whitechapel station">Whitechapel</a> the London Overground runs below the London Underground (though there are other parts of the network where this occurs, e.g.: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watford_DC_Line" title="Watford DC Line">Watford Junction to Euston route</a> between <a href="/wiki/Kenton_station" title="Kenton station">Kenton</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Kenton_station" title="South Kenton station">South Kenton</a> – shared with the <a href="/wiki/Bakerloo_line" title="Bakerloo line">Bakerloo line</a> – passes under the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_line" title="Metropolitan line">Metropolitan line</a> between <a href="/wiki/Northwick_Park_tube_station" title="Northwick Park tube station">Northwick Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Preston_Road_tube_station" title="Preston Road tube station">Preston Road</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_London_Line_extension">South London Line extension</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: South London Line extension">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/South_London_Line" title="South London Line">South London Line</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Denmark_Hill_stn_high_eastbound.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Denmark_Hill_stn_high_eastbound.JPG/220px-Denmark_Hill_stn_high_eastbound.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Denmark_Hill_stn_high_eastbound.JPG/330px-Denmark_Hill_stn_high_eastbound.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Denmark_Hill_stn_high_eastbound.JPG/440px-Denmark_Hill_stn_high_eastbound.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Denmark_Hill_stn_high_eastbound.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Denmark Hill station on the Overground South London Line extension</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The next addition opened on 9 December 2012, from <a href="/wiki/Surrey_Quays_railway_station" title="Surrey Quays railway station">Surrey Quays</a> to <a href="/wiki/Clapham_Junction_railway_station" title="Clapham Junction railway station">Clapham Junction</a> via the South London Line, calling at <a href="/wiki/Queens_Road_Peckham_railway_station" title="Queens Road Peckham railway station">Queens Road Peckham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peckham_Rye_railway_station" title="Peckham Rye railway station">Peckham Rye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark_Hill_railway_station" title="Denmark Hill railway station">Denmark Hill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clapham_High_Street_railway_station" title="Clapham High Street railway station">Clapham High Street</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wandsworth_Road_railway_station" title="Wandsworth Road railway station">Wandsworth Road</a>. The extension uses an alignment between Surrey Quays and just north of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Road_Peckham_railway_station" title="Queen's Road Peckham railway station">Queen's Road Peckham</a> that had been disused since 1911, new track was laid following some major civil engineering works. Passive provision has also been made for a new station at <a href="/wiki/New_Bermondsey_railway_station" title="New Bermondsey railway station">New Bermondsey</a>, to be constructed when funding becomes fully available. This was put on hold in 2009, although a suitable station 'foundation structure' has been built to facilitate completion in the future.</p>
<p>Funding for the railway rebuilding project was secured in February 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> including £64 million from the DfT and £15 million from TfL, with construction beginning in May 2011.</p>
<p>The route passes over both <a href="/wiki/Loughborough_Junction_railway_station" title="Loughborough Junction railway station">Loughborough Junction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brixton_railway_station" title="Brixton railway station">Brixton</a> stations without stopping, and this lack of interchange stations was criticised by local politicians during the planning phase of the project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> No stations are planned at these locations as the line is on high railway arches, making the cost of any station construction prohibitive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North-east_and_East_London">North-east and East London</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: North-east and East London">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 31 May 2015 the <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_Street_station" title="Liverpool Street station">Liverpool Street</a> to <a href="/wiki/Enfield_Town_railway_station" title="Enfield Town railway station">Enfield Town</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheshunt_railway_station" title="Cheshunt railway station">Cheshunt</a> (via <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sisters_station" title="Seven Sisters station">Seven Sisters</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Chingford_railway_station" title="Chingford railway station">Chingford</a> services, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Romford_railway_station" title="Romford railway station">Romford</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upminster_railway_station" title="Upminster railway station">Upminster</a> service, were transferred from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abellio_Greater_Anglia" title="Abellio Greater Anglia">Abellio Greater Anglia</a> to Transport for London to become part of the London Overground network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-topham-may2015_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-topham-may2015-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Network">Network</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Network">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EMPLondonOvergroundRoundel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4160" data-file-width="3120" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/EMPLondonOvergroundRoundel.jpg/220px-EMPLondonOvergroundRoundel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/EMPLondonOvergroundRoundel.jpg/330px-EMPLondonOvergroundRoundel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/EMPLondonOvergroundRoundel.jpg/440px-EMPLondonOvergroundRoundel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EMPLondonOvergroundRoundel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
London Overground signage at <a href="/wiki/Emerson_Park_railway_station" title="Emerson Park railway station">Emerson Park</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">Introduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Introduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The initial network, service levels and timetables were a continuation of Silverlink Metro services, a set of routes primarily built and electrified by the <a href="/wiki/North_London_Railway" title="North London Railway">North London</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LNWR" title="LNWR">London &amp; North Western</a> railway companies in the 19th and early 20th centuries. As the Overground name implies, the majority of the network is above ground, and it mostly consists of railway lines that connect areas outside <a href="/wiki/Central_London" title="Central London">Central London</a>, with a considerable portion of the network in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Travelcard_Zone_2" title="Travelcard Zone 2">Zone 2</a>. The network also uses <a href="/wiki/Euston_railway_station" title="Euston railway station">Euston</a> in central London, the southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terminal_station" title="Terminal station">terminus</a> of the Watford DC Line.</p>
<p>The network interchanges with the <a href="/wiki/Bakerloo_line" title="Bakerloo line">Bakerloo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_line_(London_Underground)" title="Central line (London Underground)">Central</a>, <a href="/wiki/District_line" title="District line">District</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hammersmith_%26_City_line" title="Hammersmith &amp; City line">Hammersmith &amp; City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jubilee_line" title="Jubilee line">Jubilee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_line" title="Northern line">Northern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circle_line_(London_Underground)" title="Circle line (London Underground)">Circle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_line" title="Metropolitan line">Metropolitan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Victoria_line" title="Victoria line">Victoria</a> tube lines, and also with the <a href="/wiki/Docklands_Light_Railway" title="Docklands Light Railway">Docklands Light Railway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tramlink" title="Tramlink">Tramlink</a> and <a href="/wiki/TfL_Rail" title="TfL Rail">TfL Rail</a> networks. The Overground lines appear on <a href="/wiki/Tube_map" title="Tube map">Tube maps</a> issued by TfL,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tubemap_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-tubemap-29">[29]</a></sup> and a separate map of the system is available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOmap_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-LOmap-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Much of London Overground passes through less affluent areas, and is seen as contributing to their regeneration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The North London and Gospel Oak to Barking lines were previously considered by the Transport Committee of the London Assembly to be neglected and not developed to their full potential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assembly_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-assembly-32">[32]</a></sup></p>

<p>Sunday service is similar on all routes except for Highbury &amp; Islington - West Croydon, which starts/terminates at Dalston Junction, and Dalston Junction - Clapham Junction, which is extended to/from Highbury &amp; Islington.</p>

<p>Out of the 112 stations on the London Overground network, 10 of them (seven being on the <a href="/wiki/East_London_Line" title="East London Line">East London Line</a>) are underground or sub surface.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations">Operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>LOROL has its head office and control centre in <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Cottage" title="Swiss Cottage">Swiss Cottage</a>. Rolling stock is maintained at depots at Willesden Junction and New Cross (newly built for the extended East London line). There are also sidings at Silwood Triangle (just north of New Cross depot), built in 2013–14.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Satellite locations for stabling trains include Stratford, London Euston and sidings (mainly used by London Midland), and Barking Depot in East London (a central depot for the National Express Group franchise c2c). Train crews are based at stations including Euston, Willesden Junction, Watford Junction, New Cross, Stratford and Gospel Oak. Until recently London Overground operated with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conductor_(transportation)" title="Conductor (transportation)">Conductor or Guard</a> on its North London, West London and Gospel Oak services. With the other 60% of Overground services already operated by only a <a href="/wiki/One-man_operation" title="One-man operation">driver</a>, it was decided in 2013 to convert these remaining two person operated trains to <a href="/wiki/One-man_operation" title="One-man operation">driver only</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operator">Operator</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Operator">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Overground is operated by a private company, <a href="/wiki/Arriva_Rail_London" title="Arriva Rail London">Arriva Rail London</a>. Following a model similar to that used for the <a href="/wiki/Docklands_Light_Railway" title="Docklands Light Railway">Docklands Light Railway</a>, TfL invited tenders for operation of the Overground. Unlike <a href="/wiki/National_Rail" title="National Rail">National Rail</a> operators under the franchise control of the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Transport" title="Department for Transport">Department for Transport</a>, TfL would set fares, procure rolling stock and decide service levels. The operator would take an element of revenue risk: TfL takes 90% of the revenue and 10% is retained by the operator, responsible for revenue collection.</p>
<p>The first operator, <a href="/wiki/London_Overground_Rail_Operations_Limited" title="London Overground Rail Operations Limited">London Overground Rail Operations Limited</a> (LOROL), a 50:50 joint venture between the <a href="/wiki/MTR_Corporation" title="MTR Corporation">MTR Corporation</a> of Hong Kong and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laing_Rail" title="Laing Rail">Laing Rail</a> was chosen by TfL on 19 June 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The contract was signed on 2 July 2007 for seven years with the option of a two-year extension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In preparation for the launch of the Overground, MTR Laing renamed itself London Overground Rail Operations. In February 2013, it was awarded a concession extension until 14 November 2016.</p>
<p>In April 2015, Transport for London placed a notice in the <a href="/wiki/Official_Journal_of_the_European_Union" title="Official Journal of the European Union">Official Journal of the European Union</a>, inviting expressions of interest in operating the next concession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In March 2016, TfL announced that Arriva Rail London had won the right to operate the London Overground concession, starting from 13 November 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ticketing">Ticketing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ticketing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ticketing is a mix of paper, <a href="/wiki/Oyster_card" title="Oyster card">Oyster card</a> electronic smart card and <a href="/wiki/Contactless_payment" title="Contactless payment">contactless payment</a> cards for "pay-as-you-go" travel. As with all National Rail and TfL services in London, passengers can use a <a href="/wiki/Travelcard" title="Travelcard">Travelcard</a> (daily, seven-day, monthly or annual); as on other National Rail services in London, paper single, return and cheap day return tickets priced under the zonal fare scheme are also available.</p>
<p>As part of an effort to improve safety and protect revenue, TfL has announced that it will introduce <a href="/wiki/Turnstile" title="Turnstile">ticket barriers</a> at a number of stations. The stations that did not have barriers when TfL took over have been fitted with standalone Oyster card readers similar to those at ungated Underground and DLR stations. The validators at <a href="/wiki/Blackhorse_Road_station" title="Blackhorse Road station">Blackhorse Road</a> which were needed to enter/exit the Oyster card system when changing to and from the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_line" title="Victoria line">Victoria line</a> were replaced with route validators, coloured pink to signify this. Route validators are used to show that a traveller using Oyster PAYG changed lines at that station, showing which of the possible routes was used. Typically, this avoids paying for zone 1 when the passenger did not travel into it.</p>
<p>Ticket stock is common National Rail stock as Overground services remain part of the National Rail network, but sometimes with a large TfL roundel in the centre and the repeated legend "<a href="/wiki/Rail_Settlement_Plan" title="Rail Settlement Plan">Rail Settlement Plan</a>" or on newer visions "National Rail" on a light green background. This ticket stock, coded "TFL" on the reverse, was introduced in November 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jnl-527p450_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jnl-527p450-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ticket_pricing">Ticket pricing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ticket pricing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Oyster PAYG is charged on the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oyster_Card#Pricing" title="Oyster Card">zone-based rules</a> as for the <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">Underground</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Docklands_Light_Railway" title="Docklands Light Railway">Docklands Light Railway</a>. Stations outside Greater London (except Watford Junction) are included in the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Travelcard_Zones_7-9" title="Travelcard Zones 7-9">Travelcard Zones 7-9</a>. On 2 January 2008 Acton Central was rezoned from zone 2 to 3, Hampstead Heath from 3 to 2 and Willesden Junction from 3 to 2 and 3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fares_2008_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-fares_2008-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paper tickets are charged on the same zone-based rules as for Underground and DLR paper tickets, which were expanded to take in the extra zones covered. Watford Junction has its own fare scale. Paper tickets are significantly more expensive than using Oyster PAYG.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Performance">Performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although a TfL service, the Overground is part of the National Rail network, unlike the <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">Underground</a>. The most recent figures released by <a href="/wiki/Network_Rail" title="Network Rail">Network Rail</a> (NR), for period 7 (2013/2014), showed that it had achieved 96.6% of the <a href="/wiki/Public_Performance_Measure" title="Public Performance Measure">Public Performance Measure</a> (PPM) target for punctuality and reliability set by the ORR – down 0.9 percentage points on the period last year. The moving annual average (MAA) of the PPM for the 12 months to 12 October 2013 was 96.5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PerfP7_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-PerfP7-42">[42]</a></sup> TfL, in conjunction with the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a>, has investigated the use of data from the <a href="/wiki/Oyster_card" title="Oyster card">Oyster smartcard</a> ticketing system to measure the performance of the Overground explicitly from the passenger perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the autumn 2011 National Passenger Survey, conducted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passenger_Focus" title="Passenger Focus">Passenger Focus</a>, London Overground received an overall satisfaction rating of 92%, a 7% improvement on the previous survey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> However, a survey in February 2014 by the consumer group <i><a href="/wiki/Which%3F" title="Which?">Which?</a></i> found that customer satisfaction of London Overground was at 6th place (out of 20 train operators) with a satisfaction percentage of 58%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Branding">Branding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Branding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shadwell_station_(East_London_Line)_south_entrance_April2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Shadwell_station_%28East_London_Line%29_south_entrance_April2010.jpg/220px-Shadwell_station_%28East_London_Line%29_south_entrance_April2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Shadwell_station_%28East_London_Line%29_south_entrance_April2010.jpg/330px-Shadwell_station_%28East_London_Line%29_south_entrance_April2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Shadwell_station_%28East_London_Line%29_south_entrance_April2010.jpg/440px-Shadwell_station_%28East_London_Line%29_south_entrance_April2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shadwell_station_(East_London_Line)_south_entrance_April2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The London Overground roundel and 3D lettering at Shadwell station</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Public presentation is visually associated with TfL design standards, using similar graphic design elements to those used on the Underground, drawing on the design heritage of <a href="/wiki/Frank_Pick" title="Frank Pick">Frank Pick</a>. These design standards have been applied to London Overground stations, signage, <a href="#Livery">rolling stock</a> and publicity. London Overground also uses the TfL corporate typeface, <a href="/wiki/Johnston_(typeface)" title="Johnston (typeface)">New Johnston</a>, on its signage, publicity, stationery and on its fleet of trains. In common with other TfL services, the Overground is denoted by its own colour, a vivid orange (<a href="/wiki/Pantone" title="Pantone">Pantone</a> 158C). The use of an orange colour was inherited from the former East London line prior to its transfer from Underground to Overground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tflcs4_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-tflcs4-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tubemap_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-tubemap-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-design-standards_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-design-standards-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>London Overground lines are shown on the <a href="/wiki/Tube_map" title="Tube map">tube map</a> using its modal colour of orange, and no attempt is made to distinguish between the different Overground lines by colour coding. Like the <a href="/wiki/Docklands_Light_Railway" title="Docklands Light Railway">Docklands Light Railway</a>, the Overground is represented as a double line rather than a solid single line, to distinguish it from the colour-coded Underground lines. The London Overground also uses a variant of the <a href="/wiki/London_Underground#Roundel" title="London Underground">TfL roundel</a>. TfL denotes its different transport modes such as London Underground or London Buses with variants of the roundel device using a range of colours, and the London Overground version consists of an orange ring with a blue bar. The roundel has its origins in a 1933 design by the <a href="/wiki/London_Passenger_Transport_Board" title="London Passenger Transport Board">London Passenger Transport Board</a> and has spawned many variations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tubemap_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-tubemap-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-design-basic_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-design-basic-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roundel_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-roundel-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike other <a href="/wiki/National_Rail" title="National Rail">National Rail</a> stations, London Overground stations which are not served by another National Rail operator now omit the red National Rail "double arrow" logo from signage outside the stations, using only the Overground roundel instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-design-signs_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-design-signs-49">[49]</a></sup> A few refurbished or new stations on the reopened East London line display the station name in large orange three-dimensional uppercase letters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Passenger_numbers">Passenger numbers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Passenger numbers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Passenger numbers have grown very quickly since the start of London Overground in 2007. Large jumps occurred in 2010/11 and 2011/12 due to the opening of the extensions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_London_line" title="East London line">East London line</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_London_line" title="South London line">South London line</a>. The transfer of some suburban services from London Liverpool Street in May 2015 from the Greater Anglia franchise to London Overground also distorted numbers, contributing to a very large growth between 2014/15 and 2015/16.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-topham-may2015_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-topham-may2015-28">[28]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rolling_stock">Rolling stock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=London_Overground&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Rolling stock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gospel_Oak_railway_station_MMB_13_378216.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2364" data-file-width="3618" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Gospel_Oak_railway_station_MMB_13_378216.jpg/220px-Gospel_Oak_railway_station_MMB_13_378216.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Gospel_Oak_railway_station_MMB_13_378216.jpg/330px-Gospel_Oak_railway_station_MMB_13_378216.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Gospel_Oak_railway_station_MMB_13_378216.jpg/440px-Gospel_Oak_railway_station_MMB_13_378216.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gospel_Oak_railway_station_MMB_13_378216.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_378" title="British Rail Class 378">Class 378 "Capitalstar"</a> unit 378216 departs Gospel Oak with a North London Line service to Stratford. Class 313 and Class 508 units were gradually phased out of London Overground services in favour of these units.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:London_Overground_Train_Interior.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2564" data-file-width="3265" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/London_Overground_Train_Interior.JPG/220px-London_Overground_Train_Interior.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/London_Overground_Train_Interior.JPG/330px-London_Overground_Train_Interior.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/London_Overground_Train_Interior.JPG/440px-London_Overground_Train_Interior.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:London_Overground_Train_Interior.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The interior of a Class 378, showing the longitudinal tube-style seating.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the Overground took over from Silverlink, TfL has pursued a programme of rolling-stock replacement in order to remove from service the ageing <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_Rail_electric_multiple_unit_classes#Second_generation" title="List of British Rail electric multiple unit classes">second-generation</a> EMUs and <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_150" title="British Rail Class 150">Class 150</a> DMUs it inherited from Silverlink. In 2009, <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_378" title="British Rail Class 378">Class 378 Capitalstars</a> built by <a href="/wiki/Bombardier_Transportation" title="Bombardier Transportation">Bombardier Transportation</a> were introduced on the electrified lines to replace the <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_313" title="British Rail Class 313">Class 313</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_508" title="British Rail Class 508">Class 508</a> units used previously, while the Class 150s were replaced by new <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_172" title="British Rail Class 172">Class 172 Turbostar</a> units on the non-electrified <a href="/wiki/Gospel_Oak_to_Barking_Line" title="Gospel Oak to Barking Line">Gospel Oak to Barking Line</a>. By October 2010 the new rolling stock had completely replaced the units previously operated by Silverlink.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The Class 313 EMUs and Class 150 DMUs units have been cascaded to other train operating companies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_(train_operating_company)" title="Southern (train operating company)">Southern</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_Capital_Connect" title="First Capital Connect">First Capital Connect</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Great_Western" title="First Great Western">First Great Western</a>. The Class 508 units were stored at <a href="/wiki/Eastleigh_Works" title="Eastleigh Works">Eastleigh Works</a> but were subsequently scrapped in 2013 after being deemed unfit.</p>
<p>The Class 378 trains were officially unveiled at <a href="/wiki/Willesden_Junction_station" title="Willesden Junction station">Willesden Junction</a> on 13 July 2009. They include a number of tube-style features, including longitudinal seating and increased standing room to provide a high-capacity metro service. They also benefit from walk-through carriage interiors and <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newtrains1_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-newtrains1-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newtrains2_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-newtrains2-56">[56]</a></sup> The North London Line has a base fleet of 24 four-car units, Class 378/2. However, these were delivered as three-car units (378/0), with the extended trains being introduced from September 2010, following platform extension works and delivery of the first 20 four-car units (378/1) for the East London Line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> A further 13 dual-voltage units were delivered to expand services, taking the total fleet to 57 four-car units. These trains are to be extended to five-car sets towards the end of 2014, starting with the East London Line sets.</p>
<p>The trains are leased from newly formed <a href="/wiki/Rolling_stock_operating_company" title="Rolling stock operating company">rolling stock operating company</a> (ROSCO) <a href="/wiki/QW_Rail_Leasing" title="QW Rail Leasing">QW Rail Leasing</a> until 2027. TfL planned initially to buy the new fleet outright, but in February 2008 announced that it would lease the trains in order to free up the £250 million capital cost of purchase, combined with reducing the risk of making a loss through any future sell-on of the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eight two-car Class 172/0 units, to be leased from <a href="/wiki/Angel_Trains" title="Angel Trains">Angel Trains</a>, were ordered on behalf of TfL in November 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and entered service in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mrjul10_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-mrjul10-61">[61]</a></sup> These were originally restricted to a top speed of 40 miles per hour (64 km/h) as opposed to their intended design speed of 100 mph (160 km/h),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> as it was thought there was a fault with the exhaust system requiring modifications to the original design and the already procured units. However, it transpired that the exhaust emission testing had been flawed and that there were no major problems with the units or the original design. <a href="/wiki/Railway_electrification_system" title="Railway electrification system">Electrification</a> of the Gospel Oak to Barking line was advocated by TfL, local boroughs and passenger groups, but had not been included as part of <a href="/wiki/Network_Rail" title="Network Rail">Network Rail's</a> Route Utilisation Strategy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assembly_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-assembly-32">[32]</a></sup> Electrification has now been approved, but there is serious concern that this will not be finished until 2017.</p>
<p>Before its closure to become part of the Overground, services on the East London line were operated with <a href="/wiki/London_Underground_A60_and_A62_Stock" title="London Underground A60 and A62 Stock">London Underground A60 and A62 Stock</a>.</p>
